<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

		<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage --><div ng-if="::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage" ng-class="vm.details.graphicalAbstract ? 			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">UHF RF indentification systems integration into existing processes is a major challenge for trading companies and retailers. The installation of antennas and interrogators and the portal configuration for achieving a required read rate is difficult, and in most cases, sub-optimally implemented. Therefore, it is essential to understand the relationship between achievable read rates and portal setup demands. In this paper, we describe a novel interrogator-to-tag channel model that can be used to determine the required components prior to system installations and tuning processes. Several model parameters are derived from typical portal setups by means of interrogation zone investigations with a custom-made highly sophisticated mobile field strength recorder. Results have shown that the field coverage of the interrogation zone is independent of the antenna height and we have proven that the three-ray LOS-model fits well with real life conditions. The TAG-model parameters mainly depend on the statistical field strength distribution obtained from two independent interrogation zones. Additional measurements will be required in order to find appropriate or even general values for the proposed bimodal field distribution function of the TAG-model for further measurement independent portal characterizations.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->			
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.scope -->

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Over the last years, RF identification (RFID) technology has gained a lot of attention. RFID at ultra-high frequencies (UHFs) is especially attractive for various applications in supply-chain management or logistics <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. The long-range power transportation feature and the ultra-low-power consumption property of current transponder chips enable a remotely powered communication along several meters of distance in terms of energy transport and data exchange.</p><p>In supply-chain management and other applications that rely on high-volume throughput, passive transponders are used because this type of transponder can be attached and manufactured in high volume at extremely low cost of a few cents <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>. Furthermore, an advanced protocol layer <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a> enables the inventory of hundreds of items per second <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a> having a sophisticated anticollision algorithm.</p><p>From the system perspective, a passive transponder must obtain its energy for operation from the RF field generated by an interrogator. Aside from the market relevant aspects such as low system costs and high transparency of stock inventories, UHF seams to be an appropriate frequency range for long-range passive RFID systems because of the moderate path loss of RF field propagation and acceptable insertion loss of silicon <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a>. Known issues coming from classical radio communications apply to UHF RFID as well. The finite propagation velocity of electromagnetic waves leads to compulsorily superimposition of the radiated power commonly known as multipath propagation with the consequence of signal fading and superimposed interference <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a>. Without appropriate measures, UHF RFID systems will suffer from read performance degradation and unwanted interrogation in the surrounding area.</p><p>Applications in the supply chain management, like the inventory process where the flow of pallets carrying hundreds of items through multiple gate or dock doors, must ensure high operational reliability. Missing reads cause mismatch between in-stock databases and actual item count and would make the entire identification system unacceptable for RFID deployment. In order to ensure the operational reliability, one has to identify all performance degradation factors causing interrogation errors. The results of an application specific study in passive UHF RFID systems <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a> has shown that material properties of the tagged items, beamwidth and quantity of the interrogator's transmit antennas, tag sensitivity, and position of the tag have a significant impact on the occurrence of missing reads. This study addresses also the influence of the pallet density and its absorption characteristics with respect to the electromagnetic wave propagation on field coverage and readability. Mitsugi et al. have published several studies dealing with readability degradation and field characterization related to UHF RFID applications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_1">[10]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_1">[12]</a>. All these approaches are based on an extensive evaluation of the actual recorded field measurement data, illustrating the reading performance associated with a defined tag sensitivity threshold and with the maximum permitted interrogation power of the forward link. Several theoretical and practical investigations <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref33" id="context_ref_33_1">[33]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref34" id="context_ref_34_1">[34]</a> have shown that the forward link limits the operational range and causes interrogation failures of actual UHF RFID technology in terms of tag sensitivity, standards, and regulations <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_1">[14]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_1">[27]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref28" id="context_ref_28_1">[28]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref29" id="context_ref_29_1">[29]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref30" id="context_ref_30_1">[30]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref31" id="context_ref_31_1">[31]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref32" id="context_ref_32_1">[32]</a>.</p><p>UHF RFID gates and portals are deployed in different geographical regions operating under different local regulations. In open-loop applications, goods are transported from one country to another passing different types of gates, but normally only with an individual tag attached during its production or packing process. Therefore, it is important that all gates, doors, and portals have a standard shape in order to fulfill system reliability along the entire supply chain.</p><p>In this paper, we propose a novel characterization method of UHF RFID portals based on RF field strength recording, RFID tag channel modeling, and qualification according to the derived channel model. To our knowledge, there is no UHF RFID interrogator-to-tag channel model for portal applications proposed in the literature that merges a mathematical path-loss model for mean power estimation obtained on the portal cross section of the portal setup with a statistical model (cumulative power distribution) for the available power along typical tag trajectories. This method can also be extended to doors, portals, or other high-power UHF RFID applications with comparable characteristics. The fundamental idea is illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>Proposed measurement and qualification procedure.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Interrogation Zone Measurement</h2></div><p>For system integrators or nonprofessionals, it is very important to have a low-cost and easy to handle measurement device available. The measurement equipment should fulfill specific requirements. First, it should be neutral to the ambient conditions in order that the field distribution is not affected by the measurement setup itself. Secondly, it should be capable of measuring the field characteristics along tag moving trajectories, at tag positions, on and optionally in the pallet for a better comparison to the actual available tag power. Repeatability should also be ensured in order to align measurement results properly. There are several devices proposed in the literature. An interesting measurement device is proposed that allows the determination of the field strength on and inside individual pallets <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_2">[13]</a>. This device operates as a battery powered active “tag emulator” and is part of the inventory and anticollision process of the interrogator. Therefore, multiple sensor modules can be operated simultaneously. The individual field strength measurements are encoded in the EPC number <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_2">[14]</a>, which is requested by an interrogator during the inventory process. This characteristic enables a wireless transmission of the measurement result. Another more sophisticated field strength recorder (FSR) provided by CISC <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_2">[15]</a> allows high-speed continuous data sampling of several modules mounted along a specified moving direction. Unfortunately, these devices suffer from additional drawbacks. On the one hand, the complicated cabling of sensor modules and main controller makes the handling difficult. On the other hand, the dipole measurement antenna is sensitive to material properties and may influence the measurement accuracy, especially in setups where the field strength inside pallets is of particular interest. As mentioned already, Mitsugi et al. have published a similar mobile field recorder for UHF RFID interrogation area measurement <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_2">[11]</a>.</p><p>We have adopted these ideas and have designed and developed two contactless measurement devices for internal use only <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref38" id="context_ref_38_2">[38]</a>. One of which is equipped with a dipole-based field probe and is more suitable for free-space field strength measurement, and the other one is equipped with a loop-based field probe antenna, which is more insensitive to different material properties for measurements inside pallets. See the photograph of these devices in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a> for detail.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Interrogation Zone Characterization</h2></div><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>Photograph of the NXP Semiconductors mobile FSRs. One of which is equipped with a dipole-antenna-based field probe and the other with a loop-antenna field probe.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
Basically, it is not possible to accomplish an interrogation zone characterization of all significant pallet configurations involved in terms of known performance degradations factors. Instead we propose a method that requires only free-space measurement data recorded in the appropriate interrogation zone. This data is correlated with an RFID tag channel model valid for portal applications to determine a statistical value of the expected field coverage within the interrogation zone. An additional safety margin between supposed tag sensitivity and expected field coverage ensures the readability of pallets carrying items with high RF absorption characteristics. The safety margin will come out from experimental test in real life portal application and will be treated as an additional loss added to the free-space path-loss model.<p></p><div class="section_2" id="sec3a"><h3>A. Statistical Modeling of the Interrogator-to-Tag Powering Channel</h3><p>The primary goal of the channel modeling is to identify the fading characteristics of the interrogator-to-tag energy transfer along a typical trajectory through the portal and to determine the mean path loss on the portal cross section. Many of these channel modeling approaches are based on the evaluation of the probability or cumulative distribution function (CDF) of the transfer signal amplitude over time. Rappaport <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_3a">[16]</a> can be mentioned for his pioneering work in the research of UHF signal fading characteristics in factory environments. During the investigation of UHF cellular radio, a variety of different channel models were propose for distinct indoor or outdoor scenarios <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_3a">[17]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref35" id="context_ref_35_3a">[35]</a>. These channel models are not directly applicable to UHF RFID due to its heterogeneous mode of operation. Little research is carried out in this area thus far. What is already published are channel models dealing with short-range indoor applications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3a">[19]</a> or statements that a Rician distribution fits UHF RFID applications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_3a">[18]</a> because of its strong line-of-sight mode of operation, but this assumption is not always valid because tags are not only attached on pallet surfaces.</p><p>It is not promising to apply a statistical channel model to the entire read volume since tag trajectories and power density are not considered properly. Therefore, we propose a two-step approach leading to a general channel model for portal applications. The basic idea is illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a>. The read volume is divided into two orthogonal planes defined as the TAG-plane and LOS-plane. The TAG-plane is aligned to the pallet moving path through the portal, perpendicular to the interrogation antennas, and is associated with a signal amplitude fading model, and the LOS-plane is aligned to the cross section in the center of the portal for mean power distribution estimation associated with an appropriate path-loss model. Measurements have shown that the path loss along the LOS-plane has a significant impact on the mean power distribution.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>Two-step approach to a portal channel model. The TAG-plane is aligned in tag moving trajectory providing field covering distribution, and the LOS-plane is aligned to the portal cross section for maximum mean power distribution modeling.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><div class="section_2" id="sec3a1a"><h4>1. TAG-plane fading model</h4><p>Once the tag is moving through the portal along the TAG-plane, it may pass through an RF power distribution comparable to a characteristic illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 4</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">5</a>. At the same time, the portal interrogator is continuously querying for tags. Tags will only answer if they are powered, therefore, the field coverage along their moving paths through the portal accounts for the number of successful reads. A statistical probability density function of such a characteristic can be modeled by means of a bimodal distribution function. The strong line-of-sight component occurring in the center of the gate is modeled with a log-normal distribution and the fading components outside the portal center are modeled with a normal distribution function. The general bimodal distribution function can be expressed with <disp-formula id="deqn1" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">F_{bm}(x)=pF_{1}(x)+(1-p)F_{2}(x) \eqno{\hbox{(1)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processed"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; The scaling factor is only important for the nonlinear least square fitting and has no influence on the CDF, which indicates the probability of signal strength lower than the specified threshold.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3a1b"><h4>2. LOS-plane mean power model</h4><p>The LOS-plane mean power model describes the expected mean power level of the cross section in the center of the portal. This model consists of a general path-loss model and a multiray interference model. General path-loss models are described in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_3a1b">[20]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3a1b">[21]</a>, but the Friis transmission equation <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn3" href="#deqn3" class="fulltext-link">(3)</a> is used for the present problem formulation as follows: <disp-formula id="deqn3" <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{G}_{t}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-14-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" antenna, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\lambda</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-16-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="MathJax_Preview">r</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-17-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-368" role="math"><span style="display: the target point.</p><p>On the other hand, the multiray interference model considers first-order ground reflections and total reflections property of the opposite portal chamber.</p><p>The first-order ground reflections are modeled by means of the well-known Fresnel's reflection coefficients <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn4" href="#deqn4" class="fulltext-link">(4)</a> valid for an interface The thickness of the concrete ground is taken into account according to <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref22" id="context_ref_22_3a1b">[22]</a>.</p><p>The total reflection property of the opposite portal chamber has its impact only on the free-space measurement scenario. Once passing a pallet through the gate, the line-of-sight direction is blocked and most of the energy is absorbed or reflected by the pallet. We have set up a measurement procedure to identify the absorption characteristics by detection of the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{S}_{21}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-24-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-24">{S}_{21}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> scattering parameter using a network analyzer (NA) and opposite antenna pairs.</p><p>In many cases, the opposite chamber is made of metal plates without shielding in order to achieve robustness and cost efficiency. This property must be considered during the qualification procedure since the measured power levels in an empty portal are higher due to the virtual gain of the opposite chamber. We propose to model the reflection property according to a flat metal plate with a dimension of the chamber outline. This is just a rough estimation that accounts for the worst case scenario and provides maximum safety margin with respect to the normal case of operation. The reflection property of a metal plate can be described with the well-known monostatic radar cross section (RCS) formula <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn5" href="#deqn5" class="fulltext-link">(5)</a>. This approach is also used for indoor radio channel modeling <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_3a1b">[23]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref26" id="context_ref_26_3a1b">[26]</a> and to determine a theoretical RCS value dependent on the incidence angle <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_3a1b">[24]</a> <disp-formula id="deqn5" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">G_{r}={\sigma_{\rm RCS}\over 4\pi d_{p}^{2}} \quad\sigma_{\rm RCS}={4\pi A^{2}\over\lambda^{2}} \eqno{\hbox{(5)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sigma_{\rm RCS}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-27-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-29-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-29">A</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the equivalent are of the chamber, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\lambda</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-30-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-30">\lambda</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the wavelength of the power carrier. Finally, we define a similar UHF RFID radio channel model, as proposed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref25" id="context_ref_25_3a1b">[25]</a> <disp-formula id="deqn6" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\displaylines{P_{r} = P_{t}g_{r}\left({\lambda\over 4\pi}\right)^{2} \left\vert\sqrt{G_{t}(\vartheta_{0})} {1\over r_{0}}e^{-jkr_{0}}\right.\hfill\cr\hfill \left. +\! \sqrt{G_{t}(\vartheta_{1})}\rho_{c}{1\over r_{1}}e^{-jkr_{1}} \!+\! \sqrt{G_{r}}{1\over r_{2}}e^{-jkr_{2}} \right\vert^{2} \cr\hfill\hbox{(6)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-31-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-31">\displaylines{P_{r} = P_{t}g_{r}\left({\lambda\over 4\pi}\right)^{2} \left\vert\sqrt{G_{t}(\vartheta_{0})} {1\over r_{0}}e^{-jkr_{0}}\right.\hfill\cr\hfill \left. +\! \sqrt{G_{t}(\vartheta_{1})}\rho_{c}{1\over r_{1}}e^{-jkr_{1}} \!+\! \sqrt{G_{r}}{1\over r_{2}}e^{-jkr_{2}} \right\vert^{2} \cr\hfill\hbox{(6)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\displaylines{P_{r} = P_{t}g_{r}\left({\lambda\over 4\pi}\right)^{2} \left\vert\sqrt{G_{t}(\vartheta_{0})} {1\over r_{0}}e^{-jkr_{0}}\right.\hfill\cr\hfill \left. +\! \sqrt{G_{t}(\vartheta_{1})}\rho_{c}{1\over r_{1}}e^{-jkr_{1}} \!+\! \sqrt{G_{r}}{1\over r_{2}}e^{-jkr_{2}} \right\vert^{2} \cr\hfill\hbox{(6)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{P}_{r}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-32-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-32">{P}_{r}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the power level at the target point, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{P}_{t}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-33-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-33">{P}_{t}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the transmit power of the interrogator, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\lambda</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-34-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-34">\lambda</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the wavelength of the power carrier, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{G}_{t}(\vartheta_{0,1})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-35-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-35">{G}_{t}(\vartheta_{0,1})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the appropriate interrogation antenna gain dependent on the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">E</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-36">E</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane angle, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{r}_{0,1,2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-37">{r}_{0,1,2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represents path length.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3a1c"><h4>3. Joint TAG/LOS-model</h4><p>In order to obtain a 3-D model that covers the desired interrogation zone, we propose the following approach. The TAG plane fading model describes the statistical distribution of the signal amplitude without contribution to the signal level. Referring to <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 4</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">5</a>, the maximum value of the signal amplitude will be different at different tag locations and when the mean distribution is fitted over all tag locations, all the individual signal level distributions result in one CDF associated with the overall tag readability. In contrast, the LOS plane mean power model gives a value for the maximum signal value in the portal cross section and defines the abscissa maximum of the tag readability CDF. The abscissa minimum is defined by the resolution of the FSR, equal to −40 dBm. Finally, the tag readability is obtained by application of a dedicated tag sensitivity threshold.</p></div></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Results and Pallet Readability</h2></div><p>We apply this approach to two different portal setups, one is operated according to European Union (EU) and the other according to U.S. regulations.</p><p>The EU setup consists of four individual Kathrein 25–180 circularly polarized directional antennas, 10.5-dBic 70° <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">H</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-38-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-38">H</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane 3-dB-beamwidth, and 30° <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">E</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-39-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-39">E</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane beamwidth, arranged in [0.7, 1.4, 1.4, 0.7] m from ground plus a Sirit Infinity 510 UHF RFID interrogator set to 27-dBm conductive power, 866 MHz, and continuous wave output. The U.S. setup consists of four individual Symbol Andrew RFID-900-SC high-performance area antennas, 6.0-dBi 70° in both <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">H</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-40-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-40">H</script></tex-math></inline-formula>- and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">E</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-41-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-41">E</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-plane 3-dB-beamwidth, arranged in [0.65, 1.75, 1.75, 0.65] m, and the Sirit Infinity 510 set to 27-dBm conductive power, 915 MHz, and continuous wave output.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec4a"><h3>A. TAG-Plane Fading Model</h3><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>CDF of the used EU portal derived from the FSR measurements and fitted model function showing <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{p}_{({P})}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-42-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-42">{p}_{({P})}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> as the probability of the power level greater than the threshold.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig7"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-7-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-7-small.gif" alt="Figure 7" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 7. </b><fig><p>CDF of the used U.S. portal derived from the FSR measurements and fitted model function showing <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{p}_{({P})}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-43-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-43">{p}_{({P})}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> as the probability of the power level greater than the threshold.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig8"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-8-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-8-small.gif" alt="Figure 8" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 8. </b><fig><p>LOS-plane mean power model of the EU portal setup. Antenna 3 is used as interrogation antenna and defines the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">x</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-44-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-44">x</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-axes (vertical) origin.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
According to the proposed method, the TAG-plane measurements are accomplished by means of the FSR devices. A set of 18 measurements where taken per antenna. Therefore, the FSR position is varied on an automated transportation device <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_4a">[9]</a> in dimensions of a typical pallet outline. The CDFs derived from the measurement data show similar characteristics and do not depend on the antenna position (see <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 6</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">7</a>). Hence, it can be concluded that the portal interrogation zone does not depend on the portal surroundings. It can be noted that the free-space field coverage is a bit higher in the U.S. setup, 50% achieved at around −10 dBm compared to −15 dBm achieved in the EU setup. This is probably caused by the broader antenna beamwidth used in the U.S. setup (the broader beamwidth combined with scattering from metal object surrounding the portal may generate a higher level of reflections, resulting in an increasing of the field).
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig9"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-9-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-9-small.gif" alt="Figure 9" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 9. </b><fig><p>LOS-plane mean power model of the U.S. portal setup. Antenna 3 is used as interrogation antenna and defines the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">x</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-45-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-45">x</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-axes (vertical) origin.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>U.S. FSR Results<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec4b"><h3>B. LOS-Plane Mean Power Model</h3><p>According to the proposed method, the LOS-plane mean power model is used to describe the field strength distribution on the portal cross section. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a> shows the simulation result of the EU setup where antenna 3 is the interrogation antenna, which defines the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">x</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-46-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-46">x</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-axes origin. The simulation result matches well to the real-life situation. The comparison is performed by taking the field strength values of the TAG-plane measurement data when the FSR is passing through the portal cross section. The U.S. setup is analyzed in a similar way. The simulation result is illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9</a> and shows a slightly lower mean power distribution compared to the EU setup. The comparison is not directly done on the graph to simplify the notation. The value in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig 9</a> must be compared with the measured point (given only for a few spatial positions in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>).</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec4c"><h3>C. Pallet Readability</h3><p>The pallet readability depends not only on the present field coverage of the interrogation zone and the pallet density, but also on the operational sequence of the anticollision protocol <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_4c">[3]</a>.</p><p>There has been extensive research carried out in the optimization of such ALOHA anticollision protocols <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref36" id="context_ref_36_4c">[36]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref37" id="context_ref_37_4c">[37]</a> of which impact on the reading performance is beyond the scope of this paper. A practical test of these two portals with a pallet (1.2, 1.4, 2.2 m) containing 200 tagged items has shown that the EU portal setup reaches 86.8%, whereas the U.S. portal setup reaches 80%. Referring to <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 8</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">9</a>, the mean LOS power level is about 2 dB higher in the EU compared to the U.S. setup. The pallet loss characteristic was measured and illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 10</a>. The figure describes the path loss (LOSSES) in dBm versus frequency occurring between a pair of antennas in three different conditions: free space (FS), with a low reflective pallet in the portal (“Low Reflective”), and with a “Reflective” pallet in the portal. The pallet into the portal is placed with its center corresponding to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{''moving path''}} =0\ {\hbox{m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-47-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-47">{\hbox{''moving path''}} =0\ {\hbox{m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>.</p><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{A}1\rightarrow{A}3</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-48-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-48">{A}1\rightarrow{A}3</script></tex-math></inline-formula> curve shows the path loss estimated in the center of the portal obtained by measurement using the NA connected to antenna 1 and 3 corresponding to the FS, “Low Reflective,” and “Reflective” test condition and adding a correction factor corresponding to a 1.5-m path loss to obtain the value in the center of the portal.</p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig10"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-10-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/5161480/5089411/5089411-fig-10-small.gif" alt="Figure 10" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 10. </b><fig><p><inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{S}_{21}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-49-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-49">{S}_{21}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> measurement results of a pallet with dimensions <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{1.2 m}} \times \ {\hbox{1.4 m}}\times{\hbox{2.2 m}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-50-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-50">{\hbox{1.2 m}} \times \ {\hbox{1.4 m}}\times{\hbox{2.2 m}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> by using opposite antenna pairs for radiation and reception. An NA is used to determine <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{S}_{21}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-51-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-51">{S}_{21}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> versus frequency.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5089411/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
Assuming a linear path loss (in the free-space condition) through the portal and that all four antennas are in the interrogation sequence involved, a power level of 2 dB is reached from the the pallet outline to its center The power level is obtained by adding the losses to the conductive transmitted power <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">26 - 24 = 2</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-52-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-52">26 - 24 = 2</script></tex-math></inline-formula> according to the simulation results in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig 8</a>. It is to be noted that the path loss takes into account the effect of the antenna due to the measurement setup.<p></p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec5"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION V.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>In conclusion, we have presented a novel interrogator-to-tag channel model that describes the field strength distribution in the portal interrogation zone. A custom-made FSR is used to determine the actual field strength along typical tag trajectories. The model parameters are derived from the measurement data. As the next steps, several real configurations will be examined by means of the FSR device using the present approach. The acquired data will be compared with the appropriate model predicted data to identify the level of agreement and to verify the model used in order to enable the practical use of the presented model as a tool for UHF RFID users.</p><p>Further investigations are needed on how to interpret the model parameters <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">p</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-53-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-53">p</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu_{1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-54-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-54">\mu_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sigma_{1}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-55-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-55">\sigma_{1}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\mu_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-56-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-56">\mu_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="tex"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sigma_{2}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-57-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-57">\sigma_{2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> with respect to an optimization of the portal setup, beamwidth, and selection of the antenna, etc. Furthermore, the reflection characteristics of the opposite chamber need to be studied in different setups for specifying general numbers. Based on the LOS-model, it should be possible to predict the reflection characteristics out of the measurement data. In order to predict the read rate out of the model parameters, it is essential to know absorption and reflection figures and tag locations of possible pallet configurations. These parameters are mainly customer related and no work to this subject is presented in this paper accordingly. In addition, it is essential to incorporate the influence of the anticollision algorithm in order to make a statement about the overall read rate.</p></div>
</div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

j$('document').ready(function(){
	if(getCookie('legacyUserName')!=null)
	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

In this paper, we describe a novel interrogator-to-tag channel model that can be used to determine the required components prior to system installations and tuning processes.
